restoration</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border:2px solid white;background:white;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Laurel_wreath_fa13.gif" class="image"><img alt="Laurel wreath fa13.gif" title="Portal:Hellenismos">Hellenismos&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Template:Ancient Greek religion"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Template talk:Ancient Greek religion"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Ancient_Greek_religion&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Poseidon</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/\xc9\x99/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">\xc9\x99</span><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/a\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">a\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/\xc9\x99n/: &#39;on&#39; in &#39;button&#39;">\xc9\x99n</span></span>, <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/\xc9\x92/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">\xc9\x92</span></span>-, <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/o\xca\x8a/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">o\xca\x8a</span></span>-/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%A0%CE%BF%CF%83%CE%B5%CE%B9%CE%B4%E1%BF%B6%CE%BD" class="extiw" title="wikt:\xce\xa0\xce\xbf\xcf\x83\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xb4\xe1\xbf\xb6\xce\xbd">\xce\xa0\xce\xbf\xcf\x83\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xb4\xe1\xbf\xb6\xce\xbd</a></span>, <small>pronounced&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Greek" title="Help:IPA/Greek">[pose\xcd\x9ced\xc9\x94\xcc\x81\xcd\x9c\xc9\x94n]</a></span>) was one of the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Olympians" title="Twelve Olympians">Twelve Olympians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Religion in ancient Greece">ancient Greek religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">myth</a>. He was god of the Sea and other waters; of earthquakes; and of horses.<sup id="cite_ref-Burkert1985Poseidon_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985Poseidon-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In pre-Olympian <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronze Age Greece">Bronze Age Greece</a>, he was venerated as a chief deity at <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Burkert1985Poseidon_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985Poseidon-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>  His Roman equivalent is <a href="/wiki/Neptune_(mythology)" title="Neptune (mythology)">Neptune</a>.\n</p><p>Poseidon was protector of seafarers, and of many Hellenic cities and colonies.  In <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i>, Poseidon supports the Greeks against the Trojans during the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a>. In the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>, during the sea-voyage from Troy back home to <a href="/wiki/Ithaca" title="Ithaca">Ithaca</a>, the Greek hero <a href="/wiki/Odysseus" title="Odysseus">Odysseus</a> provokes Poseidon\'s fury by blinding his son the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">Cyclops</a> <a href="/wiki/Polyphemus" title="Polyphemus">Polyphemus</a>, resulting in Poseidon punishing him with storms, the complete loss of his ship and companions, and a ten-year delay. Poseidon is also the subject of a <a href="/wiki/Homeric_hymn" class="mw-redirect" title="Homeric hymn">Homeric hymn</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>\'s <i>Timaeus</i> and <i>Critias</i>, the island of <a href="/wiki/Atlantis" title="Atlantis">Atlantis</a> was Poseidon\'s domain.<sup id="cite_ref-Plato1971Penguin_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plato1971Penguin-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Etymology"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Origins"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Origins</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Worship_of_Poseidon"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Worship of Poseidon</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Epithets"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Epithets</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Mythology"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Mythology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Birth"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Birth</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Foundation_of_Athens"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Foundation of Athens</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">List of Poseidon\'s consorts and children</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-14"><a href="#Female_lovers_and_offspring"><span class="tocnumber">5.5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Female lovers and offspring</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-15"><a href="#Male_lovers"><span class="tocnumber">5.5.2</span> <span art</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Narrations"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Narrations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Gallery"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Gallery</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>\n<p>The earliest attested occurrence of the name, written in <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a>, is <span lang="gmy" title="Mycenaean Greek language text">\xf0\x90\x80\xa1\xf0\x90\x80\xae\xf0\x90\x80\x86\xf0\x90\x80\x83</span> <i>Po-se-da-o</i> or <span lang="gmy" title="Mycenaean Greek language text">\xf0\x90\x80\xa1\xf0\x90\x80\xae\xf0\x90\x80\x86\xf0\x90\x80\xba\xf0\x90\x80\x9a</span> <i>Po-se-da-wo-ne</i>, which correspond to <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xa0\xce\xbf\xcf\x83\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xb4\xce\xac\xcf\x89\xce\xbd</span> (<i>Poseida\xc5\x8dn</i>) and <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xa0\xce\xbf\xcf\x83\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xb4\xce\xac\xcf\x9d\xce\xbf\xce\xbd\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</span> (<i>Poseidawonos</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Mycenean_Greek" class="mw-redirect" title="Mycenean Greek">Mycenean Greek</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Homeric_Greek" title="Homeric Greek">Homeric Greek</a> it appears as <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xa0\xce\xbf\xcf\x83\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xb4\xce\xac\xcf\x89\xce\xbd</span> (<i>Poseida\xc5\x8dn</i>); in <a href="/wiki/Aeolic_Greek" title="Aeolic Greek">Aeolic</a> as <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xa0\xce\xbf\xcf\x84\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xb4\xce\xac\xcf\x89\xce\xbd</span> (<i>Poteida\xc5\x8dn</i>); and in <a href="/wiki/Doric_Greek" title="Doric Greek">Doric</a> as <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xa0\xce\xbf\xcf\x84\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xb4\xce\xac\xce\xbd</span> (<i>Poteidan</i>), <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xa0\xce\xbf\xcf\x84\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xb4\xce\xac\xcf\x89\xce\xbd</span> (<i>Poteida\xc5\x8dn</i>), and <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xa0\xce\xbf\xcf\x84\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xb4\xe1\xbe\xb6\xcf\x82</span> (<i>Poteidas</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The form <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xa0\xce\xbf\xcf\x84\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xb4\xce\xac\xcf\x9d\xcf\x89\xce\xbd</span> (<i>Poteidawon</i>) appears in Corinth.<sup id="cite_ref-LSJ_Poseidon_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LSJ_Poseidon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> A common epithet of Poseidon is <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xe1\xbc\x98\xce\xbd\xce\xbf\xcf\x83\xce\xaf\xcf\x87\xce\xb8\xcf\x89\xce\xbd</span> <i>Enosichthon</i>, "Earth-shaker", an epithet which is also identified in Linear B, as  <span lang="gmy" title="Mycenaean Greek language text">\xf0\x90\x80\x81\xf0\x90\x80\x9a\xf0\x90\x80\xaf\xf0\x90\x80\x85\xf0\x90\x80\x83\xf0\x90\x80\x9a</span>, <i>E-ne-si-da-o-ne</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Earth_shaker_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earth_shaker-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> This recalls his later epithets <i>Ennosidas</i> and <i>Ennosigaios</i> indicating the chthonic nature of Poseidon.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The origins of the name "Poseidon" are unclear. One theory breaks it down into an element meaning "husband" or "lord" (Greek <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xcf\x80\xcf\x8c\xcf\x83\xce\xb9\xcf\x82</span> (<i>posis</i>), from <a href="/wiki/PIE" class="mw-redirect" title="PIE">PIE</a> <i>*p\xc3\xb3tis</i>) and another element meaning "earth" (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xb4\xe1\xbe\xb6</span> (<i>da</i>), Doric for <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xb3\xe1\xbf\x86</span> (<i>g\xc4\x93</i>)), producing something like lord or spouse of <i>Da</i>, i.e. of the earth; this would link him with <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a>, "Earth-mother".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Walter_Burkert" title="Walter Burkert">Walter Burkert</a> finds that "the second element <i>da-</i> remains hopelessly ambiguous" and finds a "husband of Earth" reading "quite impossible to prove."<sup id="cite_ref-Burkert1985Poseidon_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985Poseidon-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another theory interprets the second element as related to the word *\xce\xb4\xe1\xbe\xb6\xcf\x9d\xce\xbf\xce\xbd <i>d\xc3\xa2won</i>, "water"; this would make *<i>Posei-daw\xc5\x8dn</i> into the master of waters.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> There is also the possibility that the word has <a href="/wiki/Pre-Greek" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Greek">Pre-Greek</a> origin.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> in his dialogue <a href="/wiki/Cratylus_(dialogue)" title="Cratylus (dialogue)">Cratylus</a> gives two alternative etymologies: either the sea restrained Poseidon when walking as a "foot-bond" (\xcf\x80\xce\xbf\xcf\x83\xce\xaf\xce\xb4\xce\xb5\xcf\x83\xce\xbc\xce\xbf\xce\xbd), or he "knew many things" (\xcf\x80\xce\xbf\xce\xbb\xce\xbb\xce\xac \xce\xb5\xe1\xbc\xb0\xce\xb4\xcf\x8c\xcf\x84\xce\xbf\xcf\x82 or \xcf\x80\xce\xbf\xce\xbb\xce\xbb\xce\xac \xce\xb5\xe1\xbc\xb0\xce\xb4\xe1\xbf\xb6\xce\xbd).<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age_Greece">Bronze Age Greece</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:07Pella_Museum_Poseidon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/07Pella_Museum_Poseidon.jpg/250px-07Pella_Museum_Poseidon.jpg" width="250" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/07Pella_Museum_Poseidon.jpg/375px-07Pella_Museum_Poseidon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/07Pella_Museum_Poseidon.jpg/500px-07Pella_Museum_Poseidon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:07Pella_Museum_Poseidon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poseidon, Paella Museum</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kadriorg_Palace,_Tallinn.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kadriorg_Palace%2C_Tallinn.JPG/250px-Kadriorg_Palace%2C_Tallinn.JPG" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kadriorg_Palace%2C_Tallinn.JPG/375px-Kadriorg_Palace%2C_Tallinn.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kadriorg_Palace%2C_Tallinn.JPG/500px-Kadriorg_Palace%2C_Tallinn.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kadriorg_Palace,_Tallinn.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poseidon in <a href="/wiki/Kadriorg_Palace" title="Kadriorg Palace">Kadriorg Palace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tallinn" title="Tallinn">Tallinn</a></div></div></div>\n<h3><span id="Linear_B_.28Mycenean_Greek.29_inscriptions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Linear_B_(Mycenean_Greek)_inscriptions">Linear B  (Mycenean Greek)  inscriptions</span></h3>\n<p>If surviving Linear B <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">clay tablets</a> can be trusted, the name <i>po-se-da-wo-ne</i> ("Poseidon") occurs with greater frequency than does <i>di-u-ja</i> ("Zeus").  A feminine variant, <i>po-se-de-ia</i>, is also found, indicating a lost consort goddess,  in effect  the precursor of <a href="/wiki/Amphitrite" title="Amphitrite">Amphitrite</a>.\nPoseidon carries frequently the title <i>wa-na-ka</i> (wanax) in Linear B inscriptions, as king of the underworld.  The chthonic nature of Poseidon-Wanax is also indicated by his title <i>E-ne-si-da-o-ne</i> in Mycenean <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Earth_shaker_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earth_shaker-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> a powerful attribute (earthquakes had accompanied the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan palace-culture</a>).  In the cave of <a href="/wiki/Amnisos" title="Amnisos">Amnisos</a> (Crete) <i>Enesidaon</i> is related with the cult of Eileithyia, the goddess of childbirth.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> She was related with the annual birth of the divine child.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> During the Bronze Age, a goddess of nature, dominated both in Minoan and Mycenean cult, and <i>Wanax</i> (<i>wa-na-ka</i>) was her male companion (paredros) in Mycenean cult.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> It is possible that Demeter appears as <i>Da-ma-te</i> in a Linear B inscription (PN EN 609), however the interpretetion  is still under dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Linear B inscriptions found at <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a>, <i>E-ne-si-da-o-ne</i> is related with Poseidon, and <i>Si-to <a href="/wiki/Potnia" title="Potnia">Po-tini-ja</a></i> is probably related with Demeter.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\nTablets from <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a> record sacrificial goods destined for "the Two Queens and Poseidon" ("to the Two Queens and the King": <i>wa-na-soi</i>, <i>wa-na-ka-te</i>). The "Two Queens" may be related with <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persephone" title="Persephone">Persephone</a>, or their precursors, goddesses who were not associated with Poseidon in later periods.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arcadian_myths">Arcadian myths</span></h3>\n<p>The illuminating exception is the archaic and localised myth of <a href="/wiki/Demeter#Demeter_and_Poseidon" title="Demeter">the stallion Poseidon and mare Demeter</a> at <a href="/wiki/Phigalia" title="Phigalia">Phigalia</a> in isolated and conservative Arcadia, noted by Pausanias (2nd century AD) as having fallen into desuetude; \nthe  stallion Poseidon pursues  the mare-<a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a>, and from the union she bears  the horse Arion,  and a daughter (<a href="/wiki/Despoina" title="Despoina">Despoina</a>), who obviously had the shape of a mare too.  The violated Demeter was <i>Demeter Erinys</i> (furious) .<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>   In <a href="/wiki/Arcadia" title="Arcadia">Arcadia</a>, Demeter\'s mare-form was worshiped into historical times.  Her <i><a href="/wiki/Xoanon" title="Xoanon">xoanon</a></i> of Phigaleia shows how the local cult interpreted her, as goddess of nature.  A Medusa type with a horse\'s head with snaky hair, holding a dove and a dolphin, probably representing her power over air and water.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Poseidon_and_Athena_battle_for_control_of_Athens_-_Benvenuto_Tisi_da_Garofalo_(1512).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Poseidon_and_Athena_battle_for_control_of_Athens_-_Benvenuto_Tisi_da_Garofalo_%281512%29.jpg/220px-Poseidon_and_Athena_battle_for_control_of_Athens_-_Benvenuto_Tisi_da_Garofalo_%281512%29.jpg" width="220" height="341" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Poseidon_and_Athena_battle_for_control_of_Athens_-_Benvenuto_Tisi_da_Garofalo_%281512%29.jpg/330px-Poseidon_and_Athena_battle_for_control_of_Athens_-_Benvenuto_Tisi_da_Garofalo_%281512%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Poseidon_and_Athena_battle_for_control_of_Athens_-_Benvenuto_Tisi_da_Garofalo_%281512%29.jpg/440px-Poseidon_and_Athena_battle_for_control_of_Athens_-_Benvenuto_Tisi_da_Garofalo_%281512%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="661" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Poseidon_and_Athena_battle_for_control_of_Athens_-_Benvenuto_Tisi_da_Garofalo_(1512).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Poseidon and <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> battle for control of Athens</i> by <a href="/wiki/Benvenuto_Tisi" title="Benvenuto Tisi">Benvenuto Tisi</a> (1512)</div></div></div>\n<p>It seems that the Arcadian myth  is  related with the first Greek speaking people who entered the region during the Bronze Age.  (Linear B represents an archaic Greek dialect).  Their  religious beliefs were mixed with the beliefs of the indigenous  population.  It is possible that the Greeks did not bring with them other gods except <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eos" title="Eos">Eos</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Dioskouroi" class="mw-redirect" title="Dioskouroi">Dioskouroi</a>.  The horse (<a href="/wiki/Numina" class="mw-redirect" title="Numina">numina</a>) was related with the liquid element, and with the underworld.   Poseidon  appears as a beast  (horse), which is the river spirit of the underworld, as it usually happens in northern-European folklore,  and not unusually in Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>  Poseidon \xe2\x80\x9cWanax\xe2\x80\x9d, is the male companion  (paredros) of the goddess of nature.  In the relative <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan</a> myth,  <a href="/wiki/Pasipha%C3%AB" title="Pasipha\xc3\xab">Pasipha\xc3\xab</a> is mating with the white bull, and she bears the hybrid creature <a href="/wiki/Minotaur" title="Minotaur">Minotaur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Bull was the old pre-Olympian Poseidon.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The goddess of nature and her paredros survived in the <a href="/wiki/Eleusinian_mysteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Eleusinian mysteries">Eleusinian cult</a>, where the following words were uttered&#160;: " Mighty Potnia bore a strong son"<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the heavily sea-dependent Mycenaean culture, there is not sufficient  evidence that Poseidon was connected with the sea.  We do not know if "Posedeia" was a sea-goddess. <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a> suggest that Poseidon became lord of the sea following the defeat of his father <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Kronos</a>, when the world was divided by lot among his three sons; Zeus was given the sky, Hades the underworld, and Poseidon the sea, with the Earth and Mount Olympus belonging to all three.<sup id="cite_ref-Burkert1985Poseidon_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985Poseidon-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\nGiven Poseidon\'s connection with horses as well as the sea, and the landlocked situation of the likely Indo-European homeland, Nobuo Komita has proposed that Poseidon was originally an aristocratic Indo-European horse-god who was then assimilated to Near Eastern aquatic deities when the basis of the Greek livelihood shifted from the land to the sea, or a god of fresh waters who was assigned a secondary role as god of the sea, where he overwhelmed the original Aegean sea deities such as <a href="/wiki/Proteus" title="Proteus">Proteus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nereus" title="Nereus">Nereus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>  Conversely, Walter Burkert suggests that the Hellene cult worship of Poseidon as a horse god may be connected to the introduction of the horse and war-chariot from Anatolia to Greece around 1600 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Burkert1985Poseidon_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985Poseidon-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is almost sure that once Poseidon was worshiped as a horse, and this is evident by his cult in <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesos" class="mw-redirect" title="Peloponnesos">Peloponnesos</a>.  However he was  originally a god of the waters,  and therefore he became the "earth-shaker", because the Greeks  believed that the cause of the earthquakes  was the erosion of the rocks by the waters,  by the  rivers who they saw to  disappear  into the earth  and then to burst out again. This is what the natural philosophers   <a href="/wiki/Thales" class="mw-redirect" title="Thales">Thales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anaximenes_of_Miletus" title="Anaximenes of Miletus">Anaximenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> believed, which could not be different from the folklore belief.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>  Later, when the Myceneans travelled along the sea,  he was assigned a  role as god of the sea.\n</p><p>In any case, the early importance of Poseidon can still be glimpsed in <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>, where Poseidon rather than <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> is the major mover of events.  In Homer, Poseidon is the master of the sea.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship_of_Poseidon">Worship of Poseidon</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Poseidon_Penteskouphia_Louvre_CA452.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Poseidon_Penteskouphia_Louvre_CA452.jpg/250px-Poseidon_Penteskouphia_Louvre_CA452.jpg" width="250" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Poseidon_Penteskouphia_Louvre_CA452.jpg/375px-Poseidon_Penteskouphia_Louvre_CA452.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Poseidon_Penteskouphia_Louvre_CA452.jpg/500px-Poseidon_Penteskouphia_Louvre_CA452.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1092" data-file-height="1047" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Poseidon_Penteskouphia_Louvre_CA452.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poseidon holding a <a href="/wiki/Trident" title="Trident">trident</a>. Corinthian plaque, 550-525 BC. From Penteskouphia.</div></div></div>\n<p>Poseidon was a major civic god of several cities: in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Athens" title="History of Athens">Athens</a>, he was second only to <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> in importance, while in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Corinth" title="Ancient Corinth">Corinth</a> and many cities of <a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Magna Graecia</a> he was the chief god of the <a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">polis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Burkert1985Poseidon_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985Poseidon-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In his benign aspect, Poseidon was seen as creating new <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">islands</a> and offering calm seas. When offended or ignored, he supposedly struck the ground with his <a href="/wiki/Trident" title="Trident">trident</a> and caused <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/chaos" class="extiw" title="wikt:chaos">chaotic</a> springs, <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a>, drownings and <a href="/wiki/Ship" title="Ship">shipwrecks</a>. Sailors prayed to Poseidon for a safe voyage, sometimes drowning horses as a sacrifice;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> in this way, according to a fragmentary <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> paused at the Syrian seashore before the climactic <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">battle of Issus</a>, and resorted to prayers, "invoking Poseidon the sea-god, for whom he ordered a <a href="/wiki/Quadriga" title="Quadriga">four-horse chariot</a> to be cast into the waves."<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a>, Poseidon was one of the caretakers of the <a href="/wiki/Delphic_oracle" class="mw-redirect" title="Delphic oracle">oracle at Delphi</a> before Olympian <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> took it over. Apollo and Poseidon worked closely in many realms: in colonization, for example, Delphic Apollo provided the authorization to go out and settle, while Poseidon watched over the colonists on their way, and provided the <a href="/wiki/Holy_water" title="Holy water">lustral water</a> for the foundation-sacrifice. <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Anabasis_(Xenophon)" title="Anabasis (Xenophon)"><i>Anabasis</i></a> describes a group of <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Spartan</a> soldiers in 400\xe2\x80\x93399 BC singing to Poseidon a <a href="/wiki/Paean" title="Paean">paean</a>\xe2\x80\x94a kind of hymn normally sung for Apollo.\n</p><p>Like <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>, who inflamed the <a href="/wiki/Maenad" title="Maenad">maenads</a>, Poseidon also caused certain forms of mental disturbance. A <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocratic</a> text of ca 400 BC, <i>On the Sacred Disease</i><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> says that he was blamed for certain types of epilepsy.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epithets">Epithets</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg/320px-Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg" width="320" height="436" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg/480px-Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2c/Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="548" data-file-height="746" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, or Poseidon sculpture excavated at the <a href="/wiki/Villa_of_the_Papyri" title="Villa of the Papyri">Villa of the Papyri</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Poseidon was known in various guises, denoted by epithets. In the town of <a href="/wiki/Aegae_(Euboea)" title="Aegae (Euboea)">Aegae</a> in <a href="/wiki/Euboea" title="Euboea">Euboea</a>, he was known as <i>Poseidon Aegaeus</i> and had a magnificent temple upon a hill.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Poseidon also had a close association with horses, known under the epithet <i>Poseidon Hippios</i>, usually in Arcadia. He is more often regarded as the tamer of horses, but in some myths he is their father, either by spilling his seed upon a rock or by mating with a creature who then gave birth to the first horse.<sup id="cite_ref-Burkert1985Poseidon_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985Poseidon-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> He was closely related with the springs, and with the strike of his trident, he created springs. Many springs like <a href="/wiki/Hippocrene" title="Hippocrene">Hippocrene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aganippe" title="Aganippe">Aganippe</a> in Helikon are related with the word horse (hippos). \n(also Glukippe, Hyperippe).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\nIn the historical period, Poseidon was often referred to by the epithets <i>Enosichthon</i>, <i>Seisichthon</i> and <i>Ennosigaios</i>, and <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\x93\xce\xb1\xce\xb9\xce\xae\xce\xbf\xcf\x87\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</span> <i>Gai\xc4\x93ochos</i> all meaning "earth-shaker" and referring to his role in causing earthquakes.\n</p><p>Some other epithets of Poseidon are:<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>"Asphaleios", (\xce\xb1\xcf\x83\xcf\x86\xce\xac\xce\xbb\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xb1:safety), as protector from the earthquakes.</li>\n<li>"Helikonios", (\xce\x95\xce\xbb\xce\xb9\xce\xba\xcf\x8e\xce\xbd\xce\xb9\xce\xbf\xcf\x82) related with the mountain Helikon.</li>\n<li>"Tavreios", (\xce\xa4\xce\xb1\xcf\x8d\xcf\x81\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xbf\xcf\x82: related with the bull). There was a fest "Tavreia" in Ephesos.</li>\n<li>"Petraios" (\xce\xa0\xce\xb5\xcf\x84\xcf\x81\xce\xb1\xce\xaf\xce\xbf\xcf\x82: related with rocks) in Thessaly. He hit a rock, and the horse "Skyphios" appeared.</li>\n<li>"Epoptis"(\xce\xb5\xcf\x80\xcf\x8c\xcf\x80\xcf\x84\xce\xb7\xcf\x82: supervisor) in Megalopolis</li>\n<li>"Pelagios" in Ionia.</li>\n<li>Phykios" ( \xce\xa6\xcf\x8d\xce\xba\xce\xb9\xce\xbf\xcf\x82: related with seaweeds) in Mykonos.</li>\n<li>"Phytalmios" ( \xce\xa6\xcf\x85\xcf\x84\xce\xac\xce\xbb\xce\xbc\xce\xb9\xce\xbf\xcf\x82) related with the vegetation in Troizen, Megara, Rhodes.</li>\n<li>Epithets related with the genealogy trees: "Patrigenios", "Genethlios", "Genesios", "Pater", "Phratrios".</li>\n<li>"<a href="/wiki/Epactaeus" title="Epactaeus">Epactaeus</a>", meaning "god worshipped on the coast", in <a href="/wiki/Samos" title="Samos">Samos</a>.&lt;ef&gt;<a href="/wiki/File:PD-icon.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/62/PD-icon.svg/12px-PD-icon.svg.png" width="12" height="12" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/62/PD-icon.svg/18px-PD-icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/62/PD-icon.svg/24px-PD-icon.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="196" data-file-height="196" /></a>&#160;<cite class="citation encyclopaedia">Leonhard Schmitz (1870). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus:text:1999.04.0104:entry=epactaeus-bio-1&amp;highlight=epactaeus">"Epactaeus"</a>.  In <a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(lexicographer)" title="William Smith (lexicographer)">Smith, William</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_Greek_and_Roman_Biography_and_Mythology" title="Dictionary of Greek and Roman Biography and Mythology">Dictionary of Greek and Roman Biography and Mythology</a></i>.</cite><span .cs1-kern-right,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}</style>&lt;/ref&gt;</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology">Mythology</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrea_Doria_as_Neptun_by_Angelo_Bronzino.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Andrea_Doria_as_Neptun_by_Angelo_Bronzino.jpg/170px-Andrea_Doria_as_Neptun_by_Angelo_Bronzino.jpg" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Andrea_Doria_as_Neptun_by_Angelo_Bronzino.jpg/255px-Andrea_Doria_as_Neptun_by_Angelo_Bronzino.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Andrea_Doria_as_Neptun_by_Angelo_Bronzino.jpg/340px-Andrea_Doria_as_Neptun_by_Angelo_Bronzino.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2536" data-file-height="3508" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrea_Doria_as_Neptun_by_Angelo_Bronzino.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Andrea_Doria_as_Neptune" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrea Doria as Neptune">Andrea Doria as Neptune</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Angelo_Bronzino" class="mw-redirect" title="Angelo Bronzino">Angelo Bronzino</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth">Birth</span></h3>\n<p>Poseidon was the second son of titans <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(mythology)" title="Rhea (mythology)">Rhea</a>. In most accounts he is swallowed by Cronus at birth but later <a href="/wiki/Cronus#In_Greek_mythology_and_early_myths" title="Cronus">saved</a>, with his other brothers and sisters, by <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>.\nHowever, in some versions of the story, he, like his brother Zeus, did not share the fate of his other brother and sisters who were eaten by Cronus. He was saved by his mother Rhea, who concealed him among a flock of lambs and pretended to have given birth to a colt, which she gave to Cronus to devour.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/John_Tzetzes" title="John Tzetzes">John Tzetzes</a><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> the <i><a href="/wiki/Kourotrophos" title="Kourotrophos">kourotrophos</a></i>, or nurse of Poseidon was <a href="/wiki/Arne_(mythology)" title="Arne (mythology)">Arne</a>, who denied knowing where he was, when Cronus came searching; according to <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Poseidon was raised by the <a href="/wiki/Telchines" title="Telchines">Telchines</a> on <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>, just as Zeus was raised by the <a href="/wiki/Korybantes" title="Korybantes">Korybantes</a> on <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>.\n</p><p>According to a single reference in the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i>, when the world was divided by lot in three, Zeus received the sky, <a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a> the <a href="/wiki/Underworld" title="Underworld">underworld</a> and Poseidon the sea. In the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i> (v.398), Poseidon has a home in <i><a href="/wiki/Aegae_(Achaea)" title="Aegae (Achaea)">Aegae</a></i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation_of_Athens">Foundation of Athens</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ren%C3%A9-Antoine_Houasse_-_The_Dispute_of_Minerva_and_Neptune,_1689.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ren%C3%A9-Antoine_Houasse_-_The_Dispute_of_Minerva_and_Neptune%2C_1689.jpg/220px-Ren%C3%A9-Antoine_Houasse_-_The_Dispute_of_Minerva_and_Neptune%2C_1689.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ren%C3%A9-Antoine_Houasse_-_The_Dispute_of_Minerva_and_Neptune%2C_1689.jpg/330px-Ren%C3%A9-Antoine_Houasse_-_The_Dispute_of_Minerva_and_Neptune%2C_1689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ren%C3%A9-Antoine_Houasse_-_The_Dispute_of_Minerva_and_Neptune%2C_1689.jpg/440px-Ren%C3%A9-Antoine_Houasse_-_The_Dispute_of_Minerva_and_Neptune%2C_1689.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2900" data-file-height="2032" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ren%C3%A9-Antoine_Houasse_-_The_Dispute_of_Minerva_and_Neptune,_1689.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Dispute of <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune_(mythology)" title="Neptune (mythology)">Neptune</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9-Antoine_Houasse" title="Ren\xc3\xa9-Antoine Houasse">Ren\xc3\xa9-Antoine Houasse</a> (circa 1689 or 1706)</div></div></div>\n<p>Athena became the patron goddess of the city of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> after a competition with Poseidon. Yet Poseidon remained a numinous presence on the <a href="/wiki/Acropolis" title="Acropolis">Acropolis</a> in the form of his surrogate, <a href="/wiki/Erechtheus" title="Erechtheus">Erechtheus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Burkert1985Poseidon_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985Poseidon-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> At the dissolution festival at the end of the year in the Athenian calendar, the <a href="/wiki/Skira" title="Skira">Skira</a>, the priests of Athena and the priest of Poseidon would process <a href="/wiki/Baldachin" title="Baldachin">under canopies</a> to <a href="/wiki/Eleusis" title="Eleusis">Eleusis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> They agreed that each would give the Athenians one gift and the Athenians would choose whichever gift they preferred. Poseidon struck the ground with his trident and a spring sprang up; the water was salty and not very useful,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> whereas Athena offered them an <a href="/wiki/Olive" title="Olive">olive tree</a>.\n</p><p>The Athenians or their king, <a href="/wiki/Cecrops_I" title="Cecrops I">Cecrops</a>, accepted the olive tree and along with it Athena as their patron, for the olive tree brought <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">oil</a> and food. After the fight, infuriated at his loss, Poseidon sent a monstrous flood to the Attic Plain, to punish the Athenians for not choosing him. The depression made by Poseidon\'s trident and filled with salt water was surrounded by the northern hall of the <a href="/wiki/Erechtheum" class="mw-redirect" title="Erechtheum">Erechtheum</a>, remaining open to the air. "In <a href="/wiki/Cult_(religion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cult (religion)">cult</a>, Poseidon was identified with Erechtheus," <a href="/wiki/Walter_Burkert" title="Walter Burkert">Walter Burkert</a> noted; "the myth turns this into a temporal-causal sequence: in his anger at losing, Poseidon led his son <a href="/wiki/Eumolpus" title="Eumolpus">Eumolpus</a> against Athens and killed Erectheus."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Temple_of_Poseidon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Temple_of_Poseidon.jpg/250px-Temple_of_Poseidon.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Temple_of_Poseidon.jpg/375px-Temple_of_Poseidon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Temple_of_Poseidon.jpg/500px-Temple_of_Poseidon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Temple_of_Poseidon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sounion#Temple_of_Poseidon" title="Sounion">Temple of Poseidon</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Sounion" class="mw-redirect" title="Cape Sounion">Cape Sounion</a>, ca 440 BC</div></div></div>\n<p>The contest of Athena and Poseidon was the subject of the reliefs on the western pediment of the <a href="/wiki/Parthenon" title="Parthenon">Parthenon</a>, the first sight that greeted the arriving visitor.\n</p><p>This myth is construed by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Graves" title="Robert Graves">Robert Graves</a> and others as reflecting a clash between the inhabitants during Mycenaean times and newer immigrants. Athens at its height was a significant sea power, at one point defeating the <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Persian</a> fleet at <a href="/wiki/Salamis_Island" title="Salamis Island">Salamis Island</a> in a sea battle.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Walls_of_Troy">Walls of Troy</span></h3>\n<p>Poseidon and Apollo, having offended Zeus by their rebellion in Hera\'s scheme, were temporarily stripped of their divine authority and sent to serve King <a href="/wiki/Laomedon" title="Laomedon">Laomedon</a> of Troy. He had them build huge walls around the city and promised to reward them well, a promise he then refused to fulfill. In vengeance, before the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a>, Poseidon sent a sea monster to attack Troy. The monster was later killed by <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consorts_and_children">Consorts and children</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Poseidon_enthroned_De_Ridder_418_CdM_Paris_n2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Poseidon_enthroned_De_Ridder_418_CdM_Paris_n2.jpg/250px-Poseidon_enthroned_De_Ridder_418_CdM_Paris_n2.jpg" width="250" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Poseidon_enthroned_De_Ridder_418_CdM_Paris_n2.jpg/375px-Poseidon_enthroned_De_Ridder_418_CdM_Paris_n2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Poseidon_enthroned_De_Ridder_418_CdM_Paris_n2.jpg/500px-Poseidon_enthroned_De_Ridder_418_CdM_Paris_n2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1900" data-file-height="2533" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Poseidon_enthroned_De_Ridder_418_CdM_Paris_n2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poseidon on an Attic <a href="/wiki/Krater" title="Krater">kalyx krater</a> (detail), first half of the 5th century BC.</div></div></div>\n<p>Poseidon was said to have had many lovers of both sexes (see expandable list below). His consort was <a href="/wiki/Amphitrite" title="Amphitrite">Amphitrite</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Nymph" title="Nymph">nymph</a> and ancient sea-goddess, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Nereus" title="Nereus">Nereus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Doris_(mythology)" title="Doris (mythology)">Doris</a>. Together they had a son named <a href="/wiki/Triton_(mythology)" title="Triton (mythology)">Triton</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Merman" title="Merman">merman</a>.\n</p><p>Poseidon was the father of many heroes. He is thought to have fathered the famed <a href="/wiki/Theseus" title="Theseus">Theseus</a>.\n</p><p>A mortal woman named <a href="/wiki/Tyro" title="Tyro">Tyro</a> was married to <a href="/wiki/Cretheus" title="Cretheus">Cretheus</a> (with whom she had one son, <a href="/wiki/Aeson" title="Aeson">Aeson</a>), but loved <a href="/wiki/Enipeus_(mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Enipeus (mythology)">Enipeus</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Water_deity" class="mw-redirect" title="Water deity">river god</a>. She pursued Enipeus, who refused her advances. One day, Poseidon, filled with lust for Tyro, disguised himself as Enipeus, and from their union were born the heroes <a href="/wiki/Pelias" title="Pelias">Pelias</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neleus" title="Neleus">Neleus</a>, twin boys. Poseidon also had an affair with <a href="/wiki/Alope" title="Alope">Alope</a>, his granddaughter through <a href="/wiki/Cercyon" title="Cercyon">Cercyon</a>, his son and King of <a href="/wiki/Eleusina" class="mw-redirect" title="Eleusina">Eleusis</a>, begetting the <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">Attic</a> hero <a href="/wiki/Hippothoon" title="Hippothoon">Hippothoon</a>. Cercyon had his daughter buried alive but Poseidon turned her into the spring, Alope, near Eleusis.\n</p><p>Poseidon rescued <a href="/wiki/Amymone" title="Amymone">Amymone</a> from a lecherous <a href="/wiki/Satyr" title="Satyr">satyr</a> and then fathered a child, <a href="/wiki/Nauplius_(mythology)" title="Nauplius (mythology)">Nauplius</a>, by her.\n</p><p>After having raped <a href="/wiki/Caeneus" title="Caeneus">Caeneus</a>, Poseidon fulfilled her request and <a href="/wiki/Transsexualism" class="mw-redirect" title="Transsexualism">changed her into a male warrior</a>.\n</p><p>A mortal woman named <a href="/wiki/Cleito" class="mw-redirect" title="Cleito">Cleito</a> once lived on an isolated island; Poseidon fell in love with the <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> mortal and created a dwelling <a href="/wiki/Sanctuary" title="Sanctuary">sanctuary</a> at the top of a hill near the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">island</a> and surrounded the dwelling with rings of water and land to protect her. She gave birth to five sets of twin boys; the firstborn, Atlas, became the first ruler of <a href="/wiki/Atlantis" title="Atlantis">Atlantis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Plato1971Penguin_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plato1971Penguin-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Not all of Poseidon\'s children were human. In an archaic myth, Poseidon once pursued <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a>. She spurned his advances, turning herself into a <a href="/wiki/Mare_(horse)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mare (horse)">mare</a> so that she could hide in a herd of horses; he saw through the deception and became a <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">stallion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">captured</a> her. Their child was a <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arion_(mythology)" title="Arion (mythology)">Arion</a>, which was capable of human speech. Poseidon also had sexual intercourse with <a href="/wiki/Medusa" title="Medusa">Medusa</a> on the floor of a temple to <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gill,_N.S._2007_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gill,_N.S._2007-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeelig2002895-911_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeelig2002895-911-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Medusa was then changed into a <a href="/wiki/Monster" title="Monster">monster</a> by <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeelig2002895-911_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeelig2002895-911-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> When she was later beheaded by the hero <a href="/wiki/Perseus" title="Perseus">Perseus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chrysaor" title="Chrysaor">Chrysaor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pegasus" title="Pegasus">Pegasus</a> emerged from her neck.\n</p><p>His other children include <a href="/wiki/Polyphemus" title="Polyphemus">Polyphemus</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">cyclops</a>) and, finally, <a href="/wiki/Alebion" title="Alebion">Alebion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bergion" title="Bergion">Bergion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aloadae" title="Aloadae">Otos and Ephialtae</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Giant_(mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Giant (mythology)">giants</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Gill,_N.S._2007_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gill,_N.S._2007-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="List_of_Poseidon.27s_consorts_and_children"></span><span style="height:2em;width:2em"></td><td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td><td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td><td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td><td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td><td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td><td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td><td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td><td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td><td colspan="6" rowspan="2" style="border:2px solid black;padding:0.2em"><a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a></td></tr><tr style="height:1px;text-align:center"></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_literature_and_art">In literature and art</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JacobdeGheynII-NeptuneandAmphitrite.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/JacobdeGheynII-NeptuneandAmphitrite.jpg/250px-JacobdeGheynII-NeptuneandAmphitrite.jpg" width="250" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/JacobdeGheynII-NeptuneandAmphitrite.jpg/375px-JacobdeGheynII-NeptuneandAmphitrite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/JacobdeGheynII-NeptuneandAmphitrite.jpg/500px-JacobdeGheynII-NeptuneandAmphitrite.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1010" data-file-height="751" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JacobdeGheynII-NeptuneandAmphitrite.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Neptune and Amphitrite</i> by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_de_Gheyn_II" title="Jacob de Gheyn II">Jacob de Gheyn II</a> (late 1500s)</div></div></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Greek_art" title="Greek art">Greek art</a>, Poseidon rides a <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariot</a> that was pulled by a <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus_(mythology)" title="Hippocampus (mythology)">hippocampus</a> or by horses that could ride on the sea. He was associated with <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a> and three-pronged <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> <a href="/wiki/Spear" title="Spear">spears</a> (<a href="/wiki/Trident" title="Trident">tridents</a>). He lived in a palace on the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a> floor, made of <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">coral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">gems</a>.\n</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> Poseidon favors the Greeks, and on several occasion takes an active part in the battle against the Trojan forces. However, in Book XX he rescues <a href="/wiki/Aeneas" title="Aeneas">Aeneas</a> after the Trojan prince is laid low by <a href="/wiki/Achilles" title="Achilles">Achilles</a>.\n</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>, Poseidon is notable for his hatred of <a href="/wiki/Odysseus" title="Odysseus">Odysseus</a> who blinded the god\'s son, the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">cyclops</a> <a href="/wiki/Polyphemus" title="Polyphemus">Polyphemus</a>. The enmity of Poseidon prevents Odysseus\'s return home to <a href="/wiki/Ithaca" title="Ithaca">Ithaca</a> for many years. Odysseus is even told, notwithstanding his ultimate safe return, that to placate the wrath of Poseidon will require one more voyage on his part.\n</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i>, Neptune is still resentful of the wandering Trojans, but is not as vindictive as <a href="/wiki/Juno_(mythology)" title="Juno (mythology)">Juno</a>, and in Book I he rescues the Trojan fleet from the goddess\'s attempts to wreck it, although his primary motivation for doing this is his annoyance at Juno\'s having intruded into his domain.\n</p><p>A hymn to Poseidon included among the <a href="/wiki/Homeric_Hymn" class="mw-redirect" title="Homeric Hymn">Homeric Hymns</a> is a brief invocation, a seven-line introduction that addresses the god as both "mover of the earth and barren sea, god of the deep who is also lord of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Helicon" title="Mount Helicon">Helicon</a> and wide <a href="/wiki/Aegospotami" title="Aegospotami">Aegae</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and specifies his twofold nature as an Olympian: "a tamer of horses and a saviour of ships."\n</p><p>Poseidon appears in <i><a href="/wiki/Percy_Jackson_and_the_Olympians" class="mw-redirect" title="Percy Jackson and the Olympians">Percy Jackson and the Olympians</a></i> as the father of Percy Jackson and Tyson the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">Cyclops</a>. He also appears in the <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> television series <i><a href="/wiki/Once_Upon_a_Time_(TV_series)" title="Once Upon a Time (TV series)">Once Upon a Time</a></i> as the guest star of the second half of <a href="/wiki/Once_Upon_a_Time_(season_4)" title="Once Upon a Time (season 4)">season four</a> played by <a href="/wiki/Ernie_Hudson" title="Ernie Hudson">Ernie Hudson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hudson1_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hudson1-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In this version, Poseidon is portrayed as the father of the <a href="/wiki/Ursula_(The_Little_Mermaid)" title="Ursula (The Little Mermaid)">Sea Witch Ursula</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Narrations">Narrations</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nept%C3%BAnova_font%C3%A1na.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Nept%C3%BAnova_font%C3%A1na.jpg/170px-Nept%C3%BAnova_font%C3%A1na.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Nept%C3%BAnova_font%C3%A1na.jpg/255px-Nept%C3%BAnova_font%C3%A1na.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Nept%C3%BAnova_font%C3%A1na.jpg/340px-Nept%C3%BAnova_font%C3%A1na.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2136" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nept%C3%BAnova_font%C3%A1na.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Neptune\'s fountain in <a href="/wiki/Pre%C5%A1ov" title="Pre\xc5\xa1ov">Pre\xc5\xa1ov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>.</div></div></div>\n<dl><dt>Poseidon myths as told by story tellers</dt></dl>\n<p>Bibliography of
